<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><div class="mediaContainer" style="width:220px"><audio id="mwe_player_0" controls="" preload="none" style="width:220px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="71.27746031746" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="Accipiter_nisus.ogg" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/4/4d/Accipiter_nisus.ogg/Accipiter_nisus.ogg.mp3" type="audio/mpeg" data-title="MP3" data-shorttitle="MP3" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="96648" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/4d/Accipiter_nisus.ogg" type="audio/ogg; codecs=&quot;vorbis&quot;" data-title="Original Ogg file (142 kbps)" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="142249" /></audio></div>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Accipiter_nisus.ogg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Northern sparrowhawk call</div></div></div>
<p>The <b>Eurasian sparrowhawk</b> (<i>Accipiter nisus</i>), also known as the <b>northern sparrowhawk</b> or simply the <b>sparrowhawk</b>, is a small <a href="/wiki/Bird_of_prey" title="Bird of prey">bird of prey</a> in the family <a href="/wiki/Accipitridae" title="Accipitridae">Accipitridae</a>. Adult male Eurasian sparrowhawks have bluish grey upperparts and orange-barred underparts; females and juveniles are brown above with brown barring below. The female is up to 25% larger than the male – one of the greatest size differences between the sexes in any bird species. Though it is a predator which specialises in catching <a href="/wiki/Woodland" title="Woodland">woodland</a> birds, the Eurasian sparrowhawk can be found in any habitat and often hunts garden birds in towns and cities. Males tend to take smaller birds, including <a href="/wiki/Tit_(bird)" title="Tit (bird)">tits</a>, <a href="/wiki/Finch" title="Finch">finches</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Old_World_sparrow" title="Old World sparrow">sparrows</a>; females catch primarily <a href="/wiki/Thrush_(bird)" title="Thrush (bird)">thrushes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Starling" title="Starling">starlings</a>, but are capable of killing birds weighing 500&#160;g (18&#160;oz) or more.
</p><p>The Eurasian sparrowhawk is found throughout the temperate and subtropical parts of the <a href="/wiki/Old_World" title="Old World">Old World</a>; while birds from the northern parts of the range <a href="/wiki/Bird_migration" title="Bird migration">migrate</a> south for winter, their southern counterparts remain resident or make dispersive movements. Eurasian sparrowhawks breed in suitable woodland of any type, with the nest, measuring up to 60&#160;cm (2.0&#160;ft) across, built using twigs in a tree. Four or five pale blue, brown-spotted eggs are laid; the success of the breeding attempt is dependent on the female maintaining a high weight while the male brings her food. The chicks hatch after 33 days and fledge after 24 to 28 days.
</p><p>The probability of a juvenile surviving its first year is 34%, with 69% of adults surviving from one year to the next. Mortality in young males is greater than that of young females and the typical lifespan is four years. This species is now one of the most common birds of prey in Europe, although the population crashed after the Second World War. <a href="/wiki/Organochlorine" class="mw-redirect" title="Organochlorine">Organochlorine</a> <a href="/wiki/Insecticide" title="Insecticide">insecticides</a> used to treat seeds before sowing built up in the bird population, and the concentrations in Eurasian sparrowhawks were enough to kill some outright and incapacitate others; affected birds laid eggs with fragile shells which broke during incubation. However, its population recovered after the chemicals were banned, and it is now relatively common, classified as being of <a href="/wiki/Least_concern" class="mw-redirect" title="Least concern">least concern</a> by <a href="/wiki/BirdLife_International" title="BirdLife International">BirdLife International</a>.
</p><p>The Eurasian sparrowhawk's hunting behaviour has brought it into conflict with humans for hundreds of years, particularly <a href="/wiki/Pigeon_racing" title="Pigeon racing">racing pigeon</a> owners and people rearing <a href="/wiki/Poultry" title="Poultry">poultry</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gamebirds" class="mw-redirect" title="Gamebirds">gamebirds</a>. It has also been blamed for decreases in <a href="/wiki/Passerine" title="Passerine">passerine</a> populations. The increase in population of the Eurasian sparrowhawk coincides with the decline in house sparrows in Britain.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> Studies of racing pigeon deaths found that Eurasian sparrowhawks were responsible for less than 1%. <a href="/wiki/Falconry" title="Falconry">Falconers</a> have utilised the Eurasian sparrowhawk since at least the 16th century; although the species has a reputation for being difficult to train, it is also praised for its courage. The species features in <a href="/wiki/Teutonic_mythology" class="mw-redirect" title="Teutonic mythology">Teutonic mythology</a> and is mentioned in works by writers including <a href="/wiki/William_Shakespeare" title="William Shakespeare">William Shakespeare</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alfred,_Lord_Tennyson" title="Alfred, Lord Tennyson">Alfred, Lord Tennyson</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ted_Hughes" title="Ted Hughes">Ted Hughes</a>.
</p>

</li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-15"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Taxonomy">Taxonomy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eurasian_sparrowhawk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Taxonomy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Within the family Accipitridae, the Eurasian sparrowhawk is a member of the large <a href="/wiki/Genus" title="Genus">genus</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Accipiter" title="Accipiter">Accipiter</a></i>, which consists of small to medium-sized woodland hawks. Most of the Old World members of the genus are called sparrowhawks or goshawks.<sup id="cite_ref-itis_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-itis-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> The species' name dates back to the <a href="/wiki/Middle_English" title="Middle English">Middle English</a> word <i>sperhauk</i> and <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a> <i>spearhafoc</i>, a hawk which hunts sparrows. The <a href="/wiki/Old_Norse" title="Old Norse">Old Norse</a> name for the Eurasian sparrowhawk, <i>sparrhaukr</i>, was thought to have been coined by <a href="/wiki/Vikings" title="Vikings">Vikings</a> who encountered falconry in England.<sup id="cite_ref-Martin1992_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Martin1992-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> English folk names for the Eurasian sparrowhawk include <i>blue hawk</i>, referring to the adult male's colouration, as well as <i>hedge hawk</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-lore_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lore-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> <i>spar hawk</i>, <i>spur hawk</i> and <i>stone falcon</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-air_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-air-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Eurasian sparrowhawk was described by <a href="/wiki/Carl_Linnaeus" title="Carl Linnaeus">Carl Linnaeus</a> in his 1758 landmark <a href="/wiki/10th_edition_of_Systema_Naturae" title="10th edition of Systema Naturae">10th edition of <i>Systema Naturae</i></a>, as <i>Falco nisus</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-Linnaeus1758_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Linnaeus1758-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> but moved to its present genus by French zoologist <a href="/wiki/Mathurin_Jacques_Brisson" title="Mathurin Jacques Brisson">Mathurin Jacques Brisson</a> in 1760.<sup id="cite_ref-brisson_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brisson-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> The current <a href="/wiki/Binomial_nomenclature" title="Binomial nomenclature">scientific name</a> is derived from the <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> <i>accipiter</i>, meaning 'hawk' and <i>nisus</i>, the sparrowhawk.<sup id="cite_ref-bto_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bto-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Greek_mythology" title="Greek mythology">Greek mythology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nisos" title="Nisos">Nisus</a>, the king of <a href="/wiki/Megara" title="Megara">Megara</a>, was turned into a sparrowhawk after his daughter, <a href="/wiki/Scylla_(princess)" title="Scylla (princess)">Scylla</a>, cut off his purple lock of hair to present to her lover (and Nisus' enemy), <a href="/wiki/Minos" title="Minos">Minos</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Voltaire_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voltaire-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Eurasian sparrowhawk forms a <a href="/wiki/Superspecies" class="mw-redirect" title="Superspecies">superspecies</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Rufous-chested_sparrowhawk" class="mw-redirect" title="Rufous-chested sparrowhawk">rufous-chested sparrowhawk</a> of eastern and southern Africa, and possibly the <a href="/wiki/Madagascan_sparrowhawk" title="Madagascan sparrowhawk">Madagascan sparrowhawk</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-fl_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fl-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Geographic variation is <a href="/wiki/Cline_(biology)" title="Cline (biology)">clinal</a>, with birds becoming larger and paler in the eastern part of the range compared to the western part.<sup id="cite_ref-baker_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-baker-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Within the species itself, six <a href="/wiki/Subspecies" title="Subspecies">subspecies</a> are generally recognised:<sup id="cite_ref-fl_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fl-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-hbw158_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hbw158-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><i>A. n. nisus</i>, the nominate subspecies, was described by Linnaeus in 1758.<sup id="cite_ref-hbw158_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hbw158-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> It breeds from Europe and West Asia to western <a href="/wiki/Siberia" title="Siberia">Siberia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>; northern populations winter south to the Mediterranean, Northeast Africa, Arabia and Pakistan.</li>
<li><i>A. n. nisosimilis</i> was described by <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Tickell" title="Samuel Tickell">Samuel Tickell</a> in 1833.<sup id="cite_ref-hbw158_13-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hbw158-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> It breeds from central and eastern Siberia east to <a href="/wiki/Kamchatka" class="mw-redirect" title="Kamchatka">Kamchatka</a> and Japan, and south to northern China. This subspecies is wholly migratory, wintering from Pakistan and India eastwards through Southeast Asia and southern China to Korea and Japan; some even reach Africa. It is very similar to, but slightly larger than, the nominate subspecies.<sup id="cite_ref-Karasyov_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Karasyov-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><i>A. n. melaschistos</i> was described by <a href="/wiki/Allan_Octavian_Hume" title="Allan Octavian Hume">Allan Octavian Hume</a> in 1869.<sup id="cite_ref-hbw158_13-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hbw158-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> It breeds in mountains from <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a> through the <a href="/wiki/Himalayas" title="Himalayas">Himalayas</a> and southern <a href="/wiki/Tibet" title="Tibet">Tibet</a> to western China, and winters in the plains of South Asia. Larger<sup id="cite_ref-fl_11-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fl-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> and longer-tailed than <i>nisosimilis</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-pcr_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pcr-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> it has dark slate-coloured upperparts, and more distinct rufous barring on the underparts.<sup id="cite_ref-fl_11-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fl-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><i>A. n. wolterstorffi</i>, described by <a href="/wiki/Otto_Kleinschmidt" title="Otto Kleinschmidt">Otto Kleinschmidt</a> in 1900, is resident in <a href="/wiki/Sardinia" title="Sardinia">Sardinia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Corsica" title="Corsica">Corsica</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-hbw158_13-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hbw158-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> It is the smallest of all the subspecies,<sup id="cite_ref-baker_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-baker-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> darker on the upperparts and more barred below than the nominate subspecies.<sup id="cite_ref-bwpi_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bwpi-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><i>A. n. granti</i>, described by <a href="/wiki/Richard_Bowdler_Sharpe" title="Richard Bowdler Sharpe">Richard Bowdler Sharpe</a> in 1890,<sup id="cite_ref-hbw158_13-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hbw158-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> is confined to <a href="/wiki/Madeira" title="Madeira">Madeira</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Canary_Islands" title="Canary Islands">Canary Islands</a>. It is small and dark.<sup id="cite_ref-gensbol_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gensbol-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><i>A. n. punicus</i>, described by Erlanger in 1897,<sup id="cite_ref-hbw158_13-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hbw158-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> is resident in Northwest Africa north of the <a href="/wiki/Sahara" title="Sahara">Sahara</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-forsman_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-forsman-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> It is very similar to <i>nisus</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-bwpi_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bwpi-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> being large and pale.<sup id="cite_ref-gensbol_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gensbol-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Accipiter_nisus_punicus_MHNT.ZOO.2010.11.85.1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Accipiter_nisus_punicus_MHNT.ZOO.2010.11.85.1.jpg/220px-Accipiter_nisus_punicus_MHNT.ZOO.2010.11.85.1.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="171" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Accipiter_nisus_punicus_MHNT.ZOO.2010.11.85.1.jpg/330px-Accipiter_nisus_punicus_MHNT.ZOO.2010.11.85.1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Accipiter_nisus_punicus_MHNT.ZOO.2010.11.85.1.jpg/440px-Accipiter_nisus_punicus_MHNT.ZOO.2010.11.85.1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4252" data-file-height="3307" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Accipiter_nisus_punicus_MHNT.ZOO.2010.11.85.1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Eggs of <i>Accipiter nisus punicus</i> - <a href="/wiki/MHNT" class="mw-redirect" title="MHNT">MHNT</a></div></div></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Description">Description</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eurasian_sparrowhawk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Description">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Accipiter_nisus_-England_-immature_with_kill-8.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A sparrowhawk standing on and plucking a large grey bird" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Accipiter_nisus_-England_-immature_with_kill-8.jpg/220px-Accipiter_nisus_-England_-immature_with_kill-8.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="188" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Accipiter_nisus_-England_-immature_with_kill-8.jpg/330px-Accipiter_nisus_-England_-immature_with_kill-8.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Accipiter_nisus_-England_-immature_with_kill-8.jpg/440px-Accipiter_nisus_-England_-immature_with_kill-8.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2000" data-file-height="1705" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Accipiter_nisus_-England_-immature_with_kill-8.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A juvenile with a kill. Larger birds such as <a href="/wiki/Common_wood_pigeon" title="Common wood pigeon">common wood pigeons</a> are more often killed by the larger female Eurasian sparrowhawks; males usually hunt smaller prey.</div></div></div>
<p>The Eurasian sparrowhawk is a small bird of prey with short, broad wings and a long tail, both adaptations to manoeuvring through trees. Females can be up to 25% larger than males<sup id="cite_ref-bto_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bto-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> and weigh up to twice as much.<sup id="cite_ref-hbw56_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hbw56-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> Marked size difference in this direction is unusual in higher <a href="/wiki/Vertebrate" title="Vertebrate">vertebrates</a> but typical in <a href="/wiki/Birds_of_prey" class="mw-redirect" title="Birds of prey">birds of prey</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Wheeler1983_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wheeler1983-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> and most marked in birds of prey which hunt birds.<sup id="cite_ref-hbw56_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hbw56-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The adult male is 29–34&#160;cm (11–13&#160;in) long, with a <a href="/wiki/Wingspan" title="Wingspan">wingspan</a> of 59–64&#160;cm (23–25&#160;in)<sup id="cite_ref-bto_9-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bto-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> and a mass of 110–196&#160;g (3.9–6.9&#160;oz).<sup id="cite_ref-hbw158_13-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hbw158-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> He has slate-grey upperparts (sometimes tending to bluish), with finely red-barred underparts, which can look plain orange from a distance; his <a href="/wiki/Iris_(anatomy)" title="Iris (anatomy)">irides</a> are orange-yellow or orange-red. The female is much larger at 35–41&#160;cm (14–16&#160;in) long, with a wingspan of 67–80&#160;cm (26–31&#160;in),<sup id="cite_ref-bto_9-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bto-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> and a mass of 185–342&#160;g (6.5–12.1&#160;oz).<sup id="cite_ref-hbw158_13-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hbw158-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> She has dark brown or greyish-brown upperparts, and brown-barred underparts, and bright yellow to orange irides. The juvenile is warm brown above, with rusty fringes to the upperparts; and coarsely barred or spotted brown below, with pale yellow eyes;<sup id="cite_ref-bwpi_16-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bwpi-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> its throat has dark streaks and lacks a mesial (midline) stripe.<sup id="cite_ref-pcr_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pcr-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Eurasian sparrowhawk's pale underparts and darker upperparts are an example of <a href="/wiki/Countershading" title="Countershading">countershading</a>, which helps to break up the bird's outline. Countershading is exhibited by birds of prey which hunt birds and other fast-moving animals. The horizontal barring seen on adult Eurasian sparrowhawks is typical of woodland-dwelling predatory birds, while the adult male's bluish colour is also seen in other bird-eating raptors, including the <a href="/wiki/Peregrine_falcon" title="Peregrine falcon">peregrine falcon</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Merlin_(bird)" title="Merlin (bird)">merlin</a> and other <i>Accipiters</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-newton28_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-newton28-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cuculus_canorus_vogelartinfo.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Common cuckoo in flight, showing barred underparts" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Cuculus_canorus_vogelartinfo.jpg/220px-Cuculus_canorus_vogelartinfo.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="152" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Cuculus_canorus_vogelartinfo.jpg/330px-Cuculus_canorus_vogelartinfo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Cuculus_canorus_vogelartinfo.jpg/440px-Cuculus_canorus_vogelartinfo.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3450" data-file-height="2388" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cuculus_canorus_vogelartinfo.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Common_cuckoo" title="Common cuckoo">common cuckoo</a>'s resemblance to the Eurasian sparrowhawk helps it avoid aggression from the small birds whose nest it seeks to parasitise.</div></div></div>
<p>A study, using <a href="/wiki/Taxidermy" title="Taxidermy">stuffed</a> bird models, found that small birds are less likely to approach <a href="/wiki/Common_cuckoo" title="Common cuckoo">common cuckoos</a> (a <a href="/wiki/Brood_parasite" title="Brood parasite">brood parasite</a>) which have barred underparts like the Eurasian sparrowhawk. <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_reed_warbler" title="Eurasian reed warbler">Eurasian reed warblers</a> were found to be more aggressive to cuckoos which looked less hawk-like, meaning that the resemblance to the hawk (<a href="/wiki/Mimicry" title="Mimicry">mimicry</a>) helps the cuckoo to access the nests of potential hosts.<sup id="cite_ref-Welbergen2011_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Welbergen2011-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Eurasian sparrowhawk's small bill is used for plucking feathers and pulling prey apart, rather than killing or cutting. Its long legs and toes are an adaptation for catching and eating birds. The outer toe is "fairly long and slender"; the inner toe and back toe are relatively short and thick. The middle toe is very long and can be used to grasp objects, while a protuberance on the underside of the toe means that the digit can be closed without leaving a gap, which helps with gripping.<sup id="cite_ref-newton28_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-newton28-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The flight is a characteristic "flap-flap-glide", with the glide creating an undulating pattern.<sup id="cite_ref-collins_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-collins-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> This species is similar in size to the <a href="/wiki/Levant_sparrowhawk" title="Levant sparrowhawk">Levant sparrowhawk</a>, but larger than the <a href="/wiki/Shikra" title="Shikra">shikra</a> (the calls are however different<sup id="cite_ref-hbk_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hbk-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>); the male is only slightly larger than the merlin. Because of the overlap in sizes, the female can be confused with the similarly-sized male <a href="/wiki/Northern_goshawk" title="Northern goshawk">northern goshawk</a>, but lacks the bulk of that species. Eurasian sparrowhawks are smaller, more slender and have shorter wings, a square-ended tail and fly with faster wingbeats.<sup id="cite_ref-bwpi_16-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bwpi-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> A confusion species in China is the <a href="/wiki/Besra" title="Besra">besra</a>, although <i>A. n. melaschistos</i> is considerably larger.<sup id="cite_ref-Hackett1998_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hackett1998-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In Great Britain, Eurasian sparrowhawks living further north are bigger than their more southerly counterparts, with wing length (the most reliable indicator of body size) increasing by an average of 0.86&#160;mm (0.034&#160;in) in males, and 0.75&#160;mm (0.030&#160;in) in females, for each degree further north.<sup id="cite_ref-Wyllie1993_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wyllie1993-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Lifespan_and_demography">Lifespan and demography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eurasian_sparrowhawk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Lifespan and demography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Accipiter_nisus_9194.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Accipiter_nisus_9194.JPG/220px-Accipiter_nisus_9194.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Accipiter_nisus_9194.JPG/330px-Accipiter_nisus_9194.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Accipiter_nisus_9194.JPG/440px-Accipiter_nisus_9194.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="1536" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Accipiter_nisus_9194.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The yellow iris of this juvenile male Eurasian sparrowhawk (caught for <a href="/wiki/Bird_ringing" title="Bird ringing">ringing</a>) may turn orange in later life.</div></div></div>
<p>The oldest known wild Eurasian sparrowhawk lived more than two decades; it was found dead in Denmark 20&#160;years and 3&#160;months after having been <a href="/wiki/Bird_ringing" title="Bird ringing">ringed</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-EURING_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EURING-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> The typical lifespan is four years. Data analysis by the <a href="/wiki/British_Trust_for_Ornithology" title="British Trust for Ornithology">British Trust for Ornithology</a> shows that the proportion of juveniles surviving their first year of life is 34%; adult survival from one year to the next is 69%.<sup id="cite_ref-bto_9-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bto-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Birds in their first year of life weigh less than adults, and are especially light in the first two months after reaching independence. There is probably high mortality, especially for young males, during this time.<sup id="cite_ref-weights_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-weights-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> A study in southern Scotland suggested that the greater mortality in young male birds may be due to their smaller size and the smaller size of their prey, which means that they can "last less long between meals." Their size also means that their range of prey is restricted. It has been estimated that a female Eurasian sparrowhawk of average weight could survive for seven days without feeding – three days longer than a male of average weight.<sup id="cite_ref-Newton291_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Newton291-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A study of female Eurasian sparrowhawks found "strong evidence" that their rate of survival increased for the first three years of life, and declined for the last five to six years. <a href="/wiki/Senescence" title="Senescence">Senescence</a> (ageing) was the cause of the decline as the birds became older.<sup id="cite_ref-Newton1997_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Newton1997-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Distribution_and_habitat">Distribution and habitat</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eurasian_sparrowhawk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Distribution and habitat">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Eurasian_Sparrowhawk.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Eurasian_Sparrowhawk.JPG/220px-Eurasian_Sparrowhawk.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="268" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Eurasian_Sparrowhawk.JPG/330px-Eurasian_Sparrowhawk.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Eurasian_Sparrowhawk.JPG/440px-Eurasian_Sparrowhawk.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1642" data-file-height="2001" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Eurasian_Sparrowhawk.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Eurasian sparrowhawk in <a href="/wiki/Penza" title="Penza">Penza</a>, Russia</div></div></div>
<p>A widespread species throughout the temperate and subtropical parts of the <a href="/wiki/Old_World" title="Old World">Old World</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-forsman_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-forsman-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> the Eurasian sparrowhawk is resident or breeds in an estimated global range of 23,600,000&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (9,100,000&#160;sq&#160;mi) and had an estimated population of 1.5 million birds in 2009. Although global population trends have not been analysed, numbers seem to be stable, so it has been classified as being of <a href="/wiki/Least_concern" class="mw-redirect" title="Least concern">least concern</a> by <a href="/wiki/IUCN" class="mw-redirect" title="IUCN">IUCN</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> The race <i>granti</i>, with 100 pairs resident on <a href="/wiki/Madeira" title="Madeira">Madeira</a> and 200 pairs on the <a href="/wiki/Canary_Islands" title="Canary Islands">Canary Islands</a>, is threatened by loss of habitat, <a href="/wiki/Egg_collecting" class="mw-redirect" title="Egg collecting">egg-collecting</a> and illegal hunting, and is listed on Annex I of the <a href="/wiki/European_Commission" title="European Commission">European Commission</a> <a href="/wiki/Birds_Directive" title="Birds Directive">Birds Directive</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ec.europa.eu_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ec.europa.eu-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> It is one of the most common birds of prey in Europe, along with the <a href="/wiki/Common_kestrel" title="Common kestrel">common kestrel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Common_buzzard" title="Common buzzard">common buzzard</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-forsman_18-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-forsman-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> The Norwegian and <a href="/wiki/Albania" title="Albania">Albanian</a> populations are declining and, in many parts of Europe, Eurasian sparrowhawks are still shot. However, this low-level persecution has not affected the populations badly.<sup id="cite_ref-gensbol_17-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gensbol-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> In the UK, the population increased by 108% between 1970–2005, but saw a 1% decline over 1994–2006.<sup id="cite_ref-predation_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-predation-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> In Ireland it is the most common bird of prey, breeding even near the city centre of <a href="/wiki/Dublin" title="Dublin">Dublin</a>, where it frequents parks and large gardens.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Buffwood_4171.JPG" class="image"><img alt="Open deciduous woodland" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Buffwood_4171.JPG/220px-Buffwood_4171.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Buffwood_4171.JPG/330px-Buffwood_4171.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Buffwood_4171.JPG/440px-Buffwood_4171.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3648" data-file-height="2736" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Buffwood_4171.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Deciduous woodland is a typical breeding and hunting habitat for the Eurasian sparrowhawk.</div></div></div>
<p>This species is prevalent in most woodland types in its range, and also in more open country with scattered trees.<sup id="cite_ref-jonsson_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jonsson-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> Eurasian sparrowhawks prefer to hunt the edges of wooded areas, but migrant birds can be seen in any habitat.<sup id="cite_ref-forsman_18-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-forsman-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> The increased proportion of medium-aged stands of trees created by modern forestry techniques have benefited <i>Accipiter nisus</i>, according to a Norwegian study.<sup id="cite_ref-norway_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-norway-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> Unlike its larger relative the <a href="/wiki/Northern_goshawk" title="Northern goshawk">northern goshawk</a>, it can be seen in gardens and in urban areas<sup id="cite_ref-gbw_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gbw-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> and will even breed in city parks.<sup id="cite_ref-jonsson_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jonsson-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Eurasian sparrowhawks from colder regions of northern Europe and Asia <a href="/wiki/Bird_migration" title="Bird migration">migrate</a> south for the winter, some to <a href="/wiki/North_Africa" title="North Africa">north Africa</a> (some as far as equatorial east Africa) and India; members of the southern populations are resident or disperse. Juveniles begin their migration earlier than adults and juvenile females move before juvenile males.<sup id="cite_ref-forsman_18-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-forsman-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> Analysis of <a href="/wiki/Bird_ringing" title="Bird ringing">ringing</a> data collected at <a href="/wiki/Heligoland" title="Heligoland">Heligoland</a>, Germany, found that males move further and more often than females; of migrating birds ringed at <a href="/wiki/Kaliningrad" title="Kaliningrad">Kaliningrad</a>, Russia, the average distance moved before recovery (when the ring is read and the bird's whereabouts reported subsequently) was 1,328&#160;km (825&#160;mi) for males and 927&#160;km (576&#160;mi) for females.<sup id="cite_ref-bwpi_16-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bwpi-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A study of Eurasian sparrowhawks in southern Scotland found that ringed birds which had been raised on "high grade" <a href="/wiki/Territory_(animal)" title="Territory (animal)">territories</a> were recovered in greater proportion than birds which came from "low grade" territories. This suggested that the high grade territories produced young which survived better. The recovery rate also declined with increased elevation of the ground. After the post-fledging period, female birds dispersed greater distances than did males.<sup id="cite_ref-Newton2000_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Newton2000-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Food.2C_feeding_and_predation"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Food,_feeding_and_predation">Food, feeding and predation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eurasian_sparrowhawk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Food, feeding and predation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Accipiter_nisus_kill.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A sparrowhawk plucking a small bird" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Accipiter_nisus_kill.jpg/220px-Accipiter_nisus_kill.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="171" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Accipiter_nisus_kill.jpg/330px-Accipiter_nisus_kill.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Accipiter_nisus_kill.jpg/440px-Accipiter_nisus_kill.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1802" data-file-height="1401" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Accipiter_nisus_kill.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Eurasian sparrowhawks, like this male, discard the larger feathers from their prey before eating the flesh.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sparrowhawk_strikes_a_pigeon.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Sparrowhawk_strikes_a_pigeon.jpg/220px-Sparrowhawk_strikes_a_pigeon.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Sparrowhawk_strikes_a_pigeon.jpg/330px-Sparrowhawk_strikes_a_pigeon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Sparrowhawk_strikes_a_pigeon.jpg/440px-Sparrowhawk_strikes_a_pigeon.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4000" data-file-height="3000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sparrowhawk_strikes_a_pigeon.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sparrowhawk strikes a pigeon in a garden</div></div></div>
<p>The Eurasian sparrowhawk is a major predator of smaller woodland birds,<sup id="cite_ref-Post1996_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Post1996-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> though only 10% of its hunting attacks are successful.<sup id="cite_ref-gbb_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gbb-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> It hunts by surprise attack, using <a href="/wiki/Hedge" title="Hedge">hedges</a>, tree-belts, <a href="/wiki/Copse" class="mw-redirect" title="Copse">copses</a>, <a href="/wiki/Orchard" title="Orchard">orchards</a> and other cover near woodland areas; its choice of habitat is dictated by these requirements. It also makes use of gardens in built-up areas, taking advantage of the prey found there.<sup id="cite_ref-bwpi_16-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bwpi-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>It waits, hidden, for birds to come near, then breaks cover and flies out fast and low. A chase may follow, with the hawk even flipping upside-down to grab the victim from below or following it on foot through vegetation. It can "stoop" onto prey from a great height.<sup id="cite_ref-bwpi_16-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bwpi-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ian_Newton" title="Ian Newton">Ian Newton</a> describes seven modes of hunting used by Eurasian sparrowhawks:<sup id="cite_ref-newton102_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-newton102-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>Short-stay-perch-hunting</li>
<li>High soaring and stooping</li>
<li>Contour-hugging in flight</li>
<li>Still-hunting</li>
<li>Low quartering</li>
<li>Hunting by sound</li>
<li>Hunting on foot</li></ul>
<p>Male Eurasian sparrowhawks regularly kill birds weighing up to 40&#160;g (1.4&#160;oz) and sometimes up to 120&#160;g (4.2&#160;oz) or more; females can tackle prey up to 500&#160;g (18&#160;oz) or more. The weight of food consumed by adult birds daily is estimated to be 40–50&#160;g (1.4–1.8&#160;oz) for males and 50–70&#160;g (1.8–2.5&#160;oz) for females. During one year, a pair of Eurasian sparrowhawks could take 2,200&#160;<a href="/wiki/House_sparrow" title="House sparrow">house sparrows</a>, 600&#160;<a href="/wiki/Common_blackbird" title="Common blackbird">common blackbirds</a> or 110&#160;<a href="/wiki/Common_wood_pigeon" title="Common wood pigeon">wood pigeons</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-bwpi_16-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bwpi-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Species that feed in the open, far from cover, or are conspicuous by their behaviour or coloration, are taken more often by Eurasian sparrowhawks. For example, <a href="/wiki/Great_tit" title="Great tit">great tits</a> and house sparrows are vulnerable to attack. Eurasian sparrowhawks may account for more than 50% of deaths in certain species, but the extent varies from area to area.<sup id="cite_ref-newton137_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-newton137-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Males tend to take <a href="/wiki/Tit_(bird)" title="Tit (bird)">tits</a>, <a href="/wiki/Finch" title="Finch">finches</a>, <a href="/wiki/Old_World_sparrow" title="Old World sparrow">sparrows</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bunting_(bird)" title="Bunting (bird)">buntings</a>; females often take <a href="/wiki/Thrush_(bird)" title="Thrush (bird)">thrushes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Starling" title="Starling">starlings</a>. Larger quarry (such as <a href="/wiki/Dove" class="mw-redirect" title="Dove">doves</a> and <a href="/wiki/Magpie" title="Magpie">magpies</a>) may not die immediately but succumb during feather plucking and eating. More than 120 bird species have been recorded as prey and individual Eurasian sparrowhawks may specialise in certain prey. The birds taken are usually adults or fledglings, though chicks in the nest and <a href="/wiki/Carrion" title="Carrion">carrion</a> are sometimes eaten. Small mammals, including <a href="/wiki/Bat" title="Bat">bats</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mice" class="mw-redirect" title="Mice">mice</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Speakman1991_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Speakman1991-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> are sometimes caught but insects are eaten only very rarely.<sup id="cite_ref-bwpi_16-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bwpi-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:House_Sparrow-Mindaugas_Urbonas-1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A small grey and brown bird" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/House_Sparrow-Mindaugas_Urbonas-1.jpg/220px-House_Sparrow-Mindaugas_Urbonas-1.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/House_Sparrow-Mindaugas_Urbonas-1.jpg/330px-House_Sparrow-Mindaugas_Urbonas-1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/House_Sparrow-Mindaugas_Urbonas-1.jpg/440px-House_Sparrow-Mindaugas_Urbonas-1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2500" data-file-height="1875" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:House_Sparrow-Mindaugas_Urbonas-1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Birds that feed out in the open, like this <a href="/wiki/House_sparrow" title="House sparrow">house sparrow</a>, are more vulnerable to predation.</div></div></div>
<p>Small birds are killed on impact or when squeezed by the Eurasian sparrowhawk's foot, especially the two long claws. Victims which struggle are "kneaded" by the hawk, using its talons to squeeze and stab. When dealing with large prey species which peck and flap, the hawk's long legs help.<sup id="cite_ref-newton107_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-newton107-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> It stands on top of its prey to pluck and pull it apart.<sup id="cite_ref-newton28_21-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-newton28-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> The feathers are plucked and usually the breast muscles are eaten first. The bones are left, but can be broken using the notch in the bill. Like other birds of prey, Eurasian sparrowhawks produce <a href="/wiki/Pellet_(ornithology)" title="Pellet (ornithology)">pellets</a> containing indigestible parts of their prey. These range from 25 to 35&#160;mm (0.98 to 1.38&#160;in) long and 10–18&#160;mm (0.39–0.71&#160;in) wide and are round at one end and more narrow and pointed at the other. They are usually composed of small feathers, as the larger ones are plucked and not consumed.<sup id="cite_ref-tracks_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tracks-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><div id="mwe_player_1" class="PopUpMediaTransform" style="width:220px;" videopayload="&lt;div class=&quot;mediaContainer&quot; style=&quot;width:320px&quot;&gt;&lt;video id=&quot;mwe_player_2&quot;
