<p>During hunting, this species can fly 2–3&#160;km (1.2–1.9&#160;mi) per day. It rises above tree level mostly to display, soar above territory and to make longer journeys.<sup id="cite_ref-bwpi_16-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bwpi-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> A study in a forested area of Norway found that the mean size of the home ranges was 9.2&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (3.6&#160;sq&#160;mi) for males, and 12.3&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (4.7&#160;sq&#160;mi) for females, which was larger than studies in Great Britain had found, "probably due to lower land productivity and associated lower densities of prey species in the [Norwegian study area]".<sup id="cite_ref-norway_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-norway-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A study looked at the effect on the population of <a href="/wiki/Blue_tit" class="mw-redirect" title="Blue tit">blue tits</a> in an area where a pair of Eurasian sparrowhawks began to breed in 1990. It found that the annual adult survival rate for the tits in that area dropped from 0.485 to 0.376 (the rate in adjacent plots did not change). The size of the breeding population was not changed, but there were fewer non-breeding blue tits in the population.<sup id="cite_ref-Dhondt1998_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dhondt1998-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> In woodland, Eurasian sparrowhawks account for the deaths of a third of all young great tits;<sup id="cite_ref-gbb_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gbb-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> the two alarm calls given by great tits when mobbing a predator, and when fleeing from a nearby hawk, are within the optimum hearing range of both prey and predator; however, the high-pitched alarm call given when a distant flying Eurasian sparrowhawk is seen "can only be heard well by the tit."<sup id="cite_ref-Klump1986_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Klump1986-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> Research carried out in <a href="/wiki/Sussex" title="Sussex">Sussex</a>, England, found that the impact of Eurasian sparrowhawk predation on <a href="/wiki/Grey_partridge" title="Grey partridge">grey partridges</a> was highest when the partridge density was lowest,<sup id="cite_ref-basc_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-basc-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> while a 10-year study in Scotland found that Eurasian sparrowhawks did not select the <a href="/wiki/Common_redshank" title="Common redshank">common redshanks</a> they predated according to the waders' size or condition, probably because of the hawks' surprise-attack hunting technique.<sup id="cite_ref-Whitfield1999_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Whitfield1999-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Another study found that the risk of predation for a bird targeted by a Eurasian sparrowhawk or <a href="/wiki/Northern_goshawk" title="Northern goshawk">northern goshawk</a> increased 25-fold if the prey was infected with the blood <a href="/wiki/Parasite" class="mw-redirect" title="Parasite">parasite</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Leucocytozoon" title="Leucocytozoon">Leucocytozoon</a></i>, and birds with <a href="/wiki/Avian_malaria" title="Avian malaria">avian malaria</a> were 16 times more likely to be killed.<sup id="cite_ref-vetscite_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vetscite-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Predators">Predators</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eurasian_sparrowhawk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Predators">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Natural predators of the Eurasian sparrowhawk include the <a href="/wiki/Barn_owl" title="Barn owl">barn owl</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Tawny_owl" title="Tawny owl">tawny owl</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Northern_goshawk" title="Northern goshawk">northern goshawk</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Peregrine_falcon" title="Peregrine falcon">peregrine falcon</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Golden_eagle" title="Golden eagle">golden eagle</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Eagle_owl" class="mw-redirect" title="Eagle owl">eagle owl</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Red_fox" title="Red fox">red fox</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Stone_marten" class="mw-redirect" title="Stone marten">stone marten</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Pine_marten" class="mw-redirect" title="Pine marten">pine marten</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-historical_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-historical-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Breeding">Breeding</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eurasian_sparrowhawk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Breeding">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Accipiter_nisus_Forst_Jungfernheide_080610.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Three fluffy, white chicks in a nest" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Accipiter_nisus_Forst_Jungfernheide_080610.jpg/220px-Accipiter_nisus_Forst_Jungfernheide_080610.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Accipiter_nisus_Forst_Jungfernheide_080610.jpg/330px-Accipiter_nisus_Forst_Jungfernheide_080610.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Accipiter_nisus_Forst_Jungfernheide_080610.jpg/440px-Accipiter_nisus_Forst_Jungfernheide_080610.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3008" data-file-height="2000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Accipiter_nisus_Forst_Jungfernheide_080610.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The chicks stay on the nest until they are 24 to 28 days old.</div></div></div>
<p>The Eurasian sparrowhawk breeds in well-grown, extensive areas of woodland, often coniferous or mixed, preferring forest with a structure neither too dense nor too open, to allow a choice of flight paths. The <a href="/wiki/Bird_nest" title="Bird nest">nest</a> can be located in the fork of a tree, often near the trunk and where two or three branches begin, on a horizontal branch in the lower canopy, or near the top of a tall shrub. If available, <a href="/wiki/Conifer" class="mw-redirect" title="Conifer">conifers</a> are preferred. A new nest is built every year, generally close to the nest of the previous year, and sometimes using an old wood pigeon (<i>A. n. melaschistos</i> frequently uses the old nests of <a href="/wiki/Jungle_crow" title="Jungle crow">jungle crows</a><sup id="cite_ref-hbk_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hbk-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>) nest as a base; the male does most of the work. The structure, made of loose twigs up to 60&#160;cm (2.0&#160;ft) long, has an average diameter of 60&#160;cm (24&#160;in). When the <a href="/wiki/Egg_(biology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Egg (biology)">eggs</a> are laid, a lining of fine twigs or bark chippings is added.<sup id="cite_ref-bwpi_16-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bwpi-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>During the breeding season, the adult male Eurasian sparrowhawk loses a small amount of weight while feeding his mate before she lays eggs, and also when the young are large and require more food. The weight of the adult female is highest in May, when laying eggs, and lowest in August after the breeding cycle is complete. A study suggested that the number of eggs and subsequent breeding success are dependent on the female maintaining a high weight while the male is feeding her.<sup id="cite_ref-weights_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-weights-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Sexual_maturity" title="Sexual maturity">Sexual maturity</a> is reached at between 1–3 years.<sup id="cite_ref-hbw158_13-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hbw158-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> Most Eurasian sparrowhawks stay on the same territory for one breeding season, though others keep the same one for up to eight years. A change of mate usually triggers the change in territory. Older birds tend to stay in the same territory; failed breeding attempts make a move more likely. The birds which kept the same territories had higher nest success, though it did not increase between years; females which moved experienced more success the year after changing territory.<sup id="cite_ref-Newton2001_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Newton2001-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Eggs">Eggs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eurasian_sparrowhawk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Eggs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Accipiter_nisus_MHNT.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Accipiter_nisus_MHNT.jpg/220px-Accipiter_nisus_MHNT.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Accipiter_nisus_MHNT.jpg/330px-Accipiter_nisus_MHNT.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Accipiter_nisus_MHNT.jpg/440px-Accipiter_nisus_MHNT.jpg 2x" data-file-width="6143" data-file-height="4095" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Accipiter_nisus_MHNT.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i> Accipiter nisus</i> – <a href="/wiki/Mus%C3%A9um_de_Toulouse" title="Muséum de Toulouse">MHNT</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Oeufs002b,03.png" class="image"><img alt="An egg, white with brown speckles" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Oeufs002b%2C03.png/170px-Oeufs002b%2C03.png" decoding="async" width="170" height="214" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Oeufs002b%2C03.png/255px-Oeufs002b%2C03.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Oeufs002b%2C03.png/340px-Oeufs002b%2C03.png 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="806" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Oeufs002b,03.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Illustration of an egg. The background colour of the eggs changes from light blue to white on storage in collections.<sup id="cite_ref-Eggs1994_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eggs1994-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The eggs are pale blue with brown spots and each measure 35–46 x 28–35&#160;mm (1.4–1.8 x 1.1–1.4&#160;inches),<sup id="cite_ref-Eggs1994_53-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eggs1994-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> and weigh about 22.5&#160;g (0.79&#160;oz) of which 8% is shell in a healthy egg.<sup id="cite_ref-bto_9-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bto-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Usually a clutch of four or five eggs is laid. The eggs are generally laid in the morning with an interval of 2–3 days between each egg. If a clutch is lost, up to two further eggs may be laid that are smaller than the earlier eggs.<sup id="cite_ref-bwpi_16-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bwpi-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Young">Young</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eurasian_sparrowhawk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Young">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Altricial" class="mw-redirect" title="Altricial">altricial</a>, downy chicks hatch after 33&#160;days of <a href="/wiki/Avian_incubation" class="mw-redirect" title="Avian incubation">incubation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-bto_9-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bto-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> After hatching, the female cares for and feeds the chicks for the first 8–14&#160;days of life, and also during bad weather after that. The male provides food, up to six kills per day in the first week increasing to eight per day in the third and 10&#160;per day in the last week in the nest, by which time the female is also hunting.<sup id="cite_ref-bwpi_16-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bwpi-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>By 24–28&#160;days after hatching, the young birds start to perch on branches near the nest and take their first flight. They are fed by their parents for a further 28–30&#160;days, staying close to the nest while growing and practicing flying. At this stage they are extremely vocal, and their cries to their parents can often be heard a considerable distance away. The young hawks disperse after their parents stop provisioning them.<sup id="cite_ref-newton213_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-newton213-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> Though they receive the same amount of food, male chicks (roughly half the size of females) mature more quickly and seem to be ready to leave the nest sooner.<sup id="cite_ref-Sherley1993_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sherley1993-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> In a study in the <a href="/wiki/Forest_of_Ae" title="Forest of Ae">Forest of Ae</a>, south-west Scotland, it was found that 21% of nestlings over two days old died, with the causes of death being starvation, wet weather, predation and desertion by the parents.<sup id="cite_ref-Moss1978_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moss1978-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> The parasite <i>Leucocytozoon toddi</i> can be passed from parent to nestling at the nest, possibly because of the number of birds sharing a small space, thus allowing transmission.<sup id="cite_ref-Ashford1990_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ashford1990-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Relationship_with_humans">Relationship with humans</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eurasian_sparrowhawk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Relationship with humans">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pollutants">Pollutants</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eurasian_sparrowhawk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Pollutants">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Accipiter_nisus_-in_flight-8-4c.jpg" class="image"><img alt="In flight with wings spread, showing barring on underwing and tail" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Accipiter_nisus_-in_flight-8-4c.jpg/220px-Accipiter_nisus_-in_flight-8-4c.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Accipiter_nisus_-in_flight-8-4c.jpg/330px-Accipiter_nisus_-in_flight-8-4c.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Accipiter_nisus_-in_flight-8-4c.jpg/440px-Accipiter_nisus_-in_flight-8-4c.jpg 2x" data-file-width="500" data-file-height="500" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Accipiter_nisus_-in_flight-8-4c.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>In flight as seen from below, showing barring on underparts.</div></div></div>
<p>The Eurasian sparrowhawk population in Europe crashed in the second half of the 20th century.<sup id="cite_ref-forsman_18-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-forsman-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> The decline coincided with the introduction of <a href="/wiki/Cyclodiene" class="mw-redirect" title="Cyclodiene">cyclodiene</a> <a href="/wiki/Insecticide" title="Insecticide">insecticides</a> – <a href="/wiki/Aldrin" title="Aldrin">aldrin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dieldrin" title="Dieldrin">dieldrin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Heptachlor" title="Heptachlor">heptachlor</a> – used as <a href="/wiki/Seed_treatment" title="Seed treatment">seed dressings</a> in <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">agriculture</a> in 1956. The chemicals accumulated in the bodies of grain-eating birds and had two effects on <a href="/wiki/Top_predator" class="mw-redirect" title="Top predator">top predators</a> like the Eurasian sparrowhawk and <a href="/wiki/Peregrine_falcon" title="Peregrine falcon">peregrine falcon</a>: the shells of eggs they laid were too thin, causing them to break during incubation;<sup id="cite_ref-gbw_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gbw-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> and birds were poisoned by lethal concentrations of the insecticides.<sup id="cite_ref-walker_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-walker-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> Sub-lethal effects of these substances include irritability, <a href="/wiki/Convulsion" title="Convulsion">convulsions</a> and disorientation.<sup id="cite_ref-Walker2001_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Walker2001-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> In west Germany, around 80% of nests before the 1950s produced young, but only 54% were successful in the 1960s and '70s.<sup id="cite_ref-ibc92_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ibc92-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a>, for example, the species almost became extinct in <a href="/wiki/East_Anglia" title="East Anglia">East Anglia</a>, where the chemicals were most widely used; in western and northern parts of the country, where the pesticides were not used, there were no declines.<sup id="cite_ref-walker_58-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-walker-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society_for_the_Protection_of_Birds" title="Royal Society for the Protection of Birds">Royal Society for the Protection of Birds</a> bought its <a href="/wiki/Coombes_Valley_RSPB_reserve" title="Coombes Valley RSPB reserve">Coombes Valley</a> <a href="/wiki/Nature_reserve" title="Nature reserve">nature reserve</a> in <a href="/wiki/Staffordshire" title="Staffordshire">Staffordshire</a> because it was the only Eurasian sparrowhawk breeding site left in the <a href="/wiki/English_Midlands" class="mw-redirect" title="English Midlands">English Midlands</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-brit_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brit-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the UK, the use of cyclodienes as seed dressings for autumn-sown <a href="/wiki/Cereal" title="Cereal">cereals</a> was banned in 1975 and the levels of the chemicals present in the bird population began to fall.<sup id="cite_ref-walker_58-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-walker-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> The population has largely recovered to pre-decline levels, with an increase seen in many areas, for example northern Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-forsman_18-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-forsman-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> In Sweden, the population also decreased drastically from the 1950s, but recovered again once organochlorines were banned in the 1970s.<sup id="cite_ref-Widen1997_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Widen1997-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the UK, the failure rate at the egg stage had decreased from 17% to 6% by the year 2000, and the population had stabilised after reaching a peak in the 1990s.<sup id="cite_ref-Baillie2000_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baillie2000-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> A study of the eggs of Dutch Eurasian sparrowhawks found that contamination with <a href="/wiki/Dichlorodiphenyldichloroethylene" title="Dichlorodiphenyldichloroethylene">Dichlorodiphenyldichloroethylene</a> (DDE) – a "very persistent compound" produced when <a href="/wiki/DDT" title="DDT">DDT</a> breaks down<sup id="cite_ref-cdc_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cdc-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> – continued into the 1980s, though a decline in the number of clutches with broken eggs during the 1970s suggested decreasing levels of the chemical.<sup id="cite_ref-Burgers1986_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Burgers1986-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Body tissue samples from Eurasian sparrowhawks are still analysed as part of the Predatory Bird Monitoring Scheme conducted by the <a href="/wiki/Her_Majesty%27s_Government" class="mw-redirect" title="Her Majesty&#39;s Government">UK government's</a> <a href="/wiki/Joint_Nature_Conservation_Committee" title="Joint Nature Conservation Committee">Joint Nature Conservation Committee</a>. Although the average liver concentrations of <a href="/wiki/Polychlorinated_biphenyl" title="Polychlorinated biphenyl">polychlorinated biphenyls</a> (PCBs) in Eurasian sparrowhawks were lower in birds that died in 2005 compared to those that died in 2004, there was not a significant or consistent decline in residues between 2000–2005.<sup id="cite_ref-Walker2007_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Walker2007-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Conflict_with_human_interests">Conflict with human interests</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eurasian_sparrowhawk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Conflict with human interests">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Eurasian sparrowhawk's adaptation for feeding on birds has brought it into conflict with humans;<sup id="cite_ref-gbw_36-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gbw-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> in the 19th century it was described as "the great enemy of small quadrupeds and birds, and often very destructive to young chicks in poultry-yards in the breeding season"<sup id="cite_ref-penny_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-penny-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> and "very destructive to partridges."<sup id="cite_ref-Jenyns1835_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jenyns1835-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> Writing for <a href="/wiki/Gamekeeper" class="mw-redirect" title="Gamekeeper">gamekeepers</a> in 1851, T. B. Johnson recommended that: "The nest of this bird should be diligently sought&#160;... and destroyed, shooting the parent birds first, if possible."<sup id="cite_ref-Johnson1851_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Johnson1851-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Phasianus_colchicus_-Netherlands-8a.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Profile of a large brown bird with a green and red head and a white collar" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Phasianus_colchicus_-Netherlands-8a.jpg/220px-Phasianus_colchicus_-Netherlands-8a.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="154" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Phasianus_colchicus_-Netherlands-8a.jpg/330px-Phasianus_colchicus_-Netherlands-8a.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Phasianus_colchicus_-Netherlands-8a.jpg/440px-Phasianus_colchicus_-Netherlands-8a.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="719" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Phasianus_colchicus_-Netherlands-8a.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Eurasian sparrowhawk's natural hunting behaviour can conflict with gamekeepers rearing <a href="/wiki/Common_pheasant" title="Common pheasant">common pheasants</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>It was written in 1870 that "The sparrowhawk is perhaps only the true enemy of the game-preserver; though at the same time it is probable that if the good and evil it does were justly weighed, the balance would be in favour of the hawk, its favourite quarry being the <a href="/wiki/Common_wood_pigeon" title="Common wood pigeon">wood pigeon</a>, which is now increasing to an extent injurious to agriculture."<sup id="cite_ref-Murray1870_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Murray1870-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Parish_records" class="mw-redirect" title="Parish records">Parish records</a> of the 18th century for <a href="/wiki/Aldworth" title="Aldworth">Aldworth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Berkshire" title="Berkshire">Berkshire</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Southern_England" title="Southern England">southern England</a>, show that payments were made for 106 Eurasian sparrowhawks' heads, at the same time as efforts were being made to control the numbers of <a href="/wiki/Old_World_sparrow" title="Old World sparrow">sparrows</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-silentfields_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-silentfields-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The species suffered heavy persecution by 18th-century European landowners and gamekeepers, but withstood attempts to eradicate it. For example, on the estate at <a href="/wiki/Sandringham_House" title="Sandringham House">Sandringham</a> in <a href="/wiki/Norfolk" title="Norfolk">Norfolk</a>, 1,645 'hawks' were killed between 1938 and 1950, with 1,115 taken between 1919 and 1926 at Langwell and Sandside in <a href="/wiki/Caithness" title="Caithness">Caithness</a>, Scotland.<sup id="cite_ref-silentfields_71-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-silentfields-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The population was able to quickly replace lost birds – there is a high proportion of non-breeding, non-territorial birds able to fill vacant territories. The habitat conserved with gamebirds in mind also suited this species and its prey; gamekeepers' more successful efforts to wipe out the <a href="/wiki/Northern_goshawk" title="Northern goshawk">northern goshawk</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pine_marten" class="mw-redirect" title="Pine marten">pine marten</a> – predators of the Eurasian sparrowhawk – may have benefited it.<sup id="cite_ref-historical_51-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-historical-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> The population increased markedly when this pressure was relaxed, for example during the <a href="/wiki/First_World_War" class="mw-redirect" title="First World War">First</a> and <a href="/wiki/Second_World_War" class="mw-redirect" title="Second World War">Second World Wars</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-bwpi_16-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bwpi-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the United Kingdom, research into the effect of predators on bird populations has been "a contentious issue," with "perceived conflict between the interests of nature conservationists and those involved in game shooting." Declines in the populations of some British <a href="/wiki/Passerine" title="Passerine">songbirds</a> since the 1960s have coincided with considerable changes in agricultural practices and also large increases in the numbers of Eurasian sparrowhawks and <a href="/wiki/European_magpie" class="mw-redirect" title="European magpie">European magpies</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-declines_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-declines-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> When the Eurasian sparrowhawk population declined because of organochlorine use, there was no great increase in the populations of songbirds. In a 1949–1979 study of 13 passerine species breeding in a 40-acre (16-hectare) oakwood at <a href="/wiki/Bookham_Commons" title="Bookham Commons">Bookham Commons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Surrey" title="Surrey">Surrey</a>, England, none was present in significantly greater numbers when Eurasian sparrowhawks were absent from the wood.<sup id="cite_ref-predation_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-predation-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Many studies, mostly short-term, failed to find an effect on songbird populations caused by predatory birds such as Eurasian sparrowhawks. But analysis of long-term, large-scale national data from the UK's Common Bird Census demonstrated that the declines in farmland songbird populations since the 1960s are unlikely to have been caused by increased predation by Eurasian sparrowhawks and magpies. The results of the study indicated that patterns of year-to-year songbird population change were the same at different sites, whether the predators were present or not.<sup id="cite_ref-declines_72-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-declines-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> Another study, which examined the effects of predators – including the Eurasian sparrowhawk and <a href="/wiki/Introduced_species" title="Introduced species">introduced</a> <a href="/wiki/Eastern_gray_squirrel" title="Eastern gray squirrel">grey squirrel</a> – on UK passerine populations, found that "whilst a small number of associations may suggest significant negative effects between predator and prey species, for the majority of the songbird species examined there is no evidence that increases in common avian predators or grey squirrels are associated with large-scale population declines."<sup id="cite_ref-bto_news_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bto_news-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Racing_pigeon_rataedl.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Grey and white pigeon" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Racing_pigeon_rataedl.jpg/170px-Racing_pigeon_rataedl.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="190" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Racing_pigeon_rataedl.jpg/255px-Racing_pigeon_rataedl.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Racing_pigeon_rataedl.jpg/340px-Racing_pigeon_rataedl.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1144" data-file-height="1280" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Racing_pigeon_rataedl.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Eurasian sparrowhawks are responsible for less than 1% of <a href="/wiki/Pigeon_racing" title="Pigeon racing">racing pigeon</a> losses.</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Pigeon_racing" title="Pigeon racing">Racing pigeon</a> <a href="/wiki/Pigeon_keeping" title="Pigeon keeping">owners</a> in Great Britain have said for many years that Eurasian sparrowhawks and <a href="/wiki/Peregrine_falcon" title="Peregrine falcon">peregrine falcons</a> "cause serious and escalating losses" of pigeons<sup id="cite_ref-basc_48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-basc-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> and some have called for these birds of prey to be killed or removed from areas surrounding homing pigeon lofts.<sup id="cite_ref-RSPB2008_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RSPB2008-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-telegraph2001_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-telegraph2001-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a>, a two-year study published in 2004, and funded by <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Natural_Heritage" class="mw-redirect" title="Scottish Natural Heritage">Scottish Natural Heritage</a> and the Scottish Homing Union (SHU), found there was "no evidence that birds of prey cause major losses of racing pigeons at lofts or during races." It reported that 56% of <a href="/wiki/Pigeon_racing" title="Pigeon racing">racing pigeons</a> were lost each year but that the proportion taken by Eurasian sparrowhawks – "often blamed for major losses" – was less than 1%, with at least 2% taken by peregrine falcons. The study was carried out by the <a href="/wiki/Central_Science_Laboratory" title="Central Science Laboratory">Central Science Laboratory</a>; researchers worked with SHU members who provided data, information on pigeon rings found at peregrine falcon nests and pigeon carcasses.<sup id="cite_ref-SCH_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SCH-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>From January to April 2009,<sup id="cite_ref-results_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-results-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Government" title="Scottish Government">Scottish Government</a> conducted a trial translocation of Eurasian sparrowhawks from around racing pigeon lofts in <a href="/wiki/Glasgow" title="Glasgow">Glasgow</a>, <a href="/wiki/Edinburgh" title="Edinburgh">Edinburgh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kilmarnock" title="Kilmarnock">Kilmarnock</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stirling" title="Stirling">Stirling</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dumfries" title="Dumfries">Dumfries</a>. The trial, which cost £25,000, was supported by the Scottish Homing Union, representing the country's 3,500 pigeon fanciers.<sup id="cite_ref-sundayherald_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sundayherald-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> The experiment was originally scheduled for early in 2008 but was postponed because it would have impinged on the birds' breeding season. It was criticised by the government's own ecological adviser, Dr Ian Bainbridge, the government body <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Natural_Heritage" class="mw-redirect" title="Scottish Natural Heritage">Scottish Natural Heritage</a><sup id="cite_ref-snh.org.uk_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-snh.org.uk-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> and organisations including the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society_for_the_Protection_of_Birds" title="Royal Society for the Protection of Birds">Royal Society for the Protection of Birds</a><sup id="cite_ref-RSPB_Scotland_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RSPB_Scotland-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Society_for_Prevention_of_Cruelty_to_Animals" title="Scottish Society for Prevention of Cruelty to Animals">Scottish Society for Prevention of Cruelty to Animals</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-sundayherald_edwards_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sundayherald_edwards-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The findings, released in January 2010,<sup id="cite_ref-scotland.gov.uk_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-scotland.gov.uk-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> showed that only seven Eurasian sparrowhawks had been removed from the area from five pigeon lofts. One hawk returned twice to the area of the loft, while new birds began to visit two other lofts. The report found that "The quantity and quality of the observational data collected meant that it was impossible to draw any firm conclusions" and the government stated that "no further research involving the trapping or translocation of raptors" would take place, while the SHU maintained that it was "very optimistic that licensed trapping and translocation could at last provide some protection."<sup id="cite_ref-results_77-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-results-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Falconry">Falconry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eurasian_sparrowhawk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Falconry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Georgian_falconers_with_sparrowhawks.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Three men with Eurasian sparrowhawks perching on their fists" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Georgian_falconers_with_sparrowhawks.jpg/220px-Georgian_falconers_with_sparrowhawks.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="141" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Georgian_falconers_with_sparrowhawks.jpg/330px-Georgian_falconers_with_sparrowhawks.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Georgian_falconers_with_sparrowhawks.jpg/440px-Georgian_falconers_with_sparrowhawks.jpg 2x" data-file-width="770" data-file-height="492" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Georgian_falconers_with_sparrowhawks.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Eurasian sparrowhawk is popular with <a href="/wiki/Falconry" title="Falconry">falconers</a> in <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(country)" title="Georgia (country)">Georgia</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The Eurasian sparrowhawk has been used in <a href="/wiki/Falconry" title="Falconry">falconry</a> for centuries and was favoured by Emperor <a href="/wiki/Akbar_the_Great" class="mw-redirect" title="Akbar the Great">Akbar the Great</a> (1542–1605) of the <a href="/wiki/Mughal_Empire" title="Mughal Empire">Mughal Empire</a>. There is a tradition of using migrant Eurasian sparrowhawks to catch <a href="/wiki/Common_quail" title="Common quail">common quail</a> in <a href="/wiki/Tunisia" title="Tunisia">Tunisia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(country)" title="Georgia (country)">Georgia</a>, where there are 500 registered <i>bazieri</i> (sparrowhawkers) and a monument to <i>bazieri</i> in the city of <a href="/wiki/Poti" title="Poti">Poti</a>. Eurasian sparrowhawks are also popular in Ireland.<sup id="cite_ref-i-a-f.org_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-i-a-f.org-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> At <a href="/wiki/Cap_Bon" class="mw-redirect" title="Cap Bon">Cap Bon</a> in <a href="/wiki/Tunisia" title="Tunisia">Tunisia</a>, and in <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>, thousands are captured each year by falconers and used for hunting migrant <a href="/wiki/Common_quail" title="Common quail">common quails</a>. Although they were formerly released at the end of the season, many are now kept because of the scarcity of migrants.<sup id="cite_ref-ibc92_60-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ibc92-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 17th century England, the Eurasian sparrowhawk was used by priests, reflecting their lowly status;<sup id="cite_ref-pbs.org_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pbs.org-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Encarta_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Encarta-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> whereas in the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>, they were favoured by ladies of noble and royal status because of their small size.<sup id="cite_ref-britishfalconersclub_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-britishfalconersclub-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> The falconer's name for a male Eurasian sparrowhawk is a "musket"; this is derived from the Latin word <i>musca</i>, meaning 'a fly', via the <a href="/wiki/Old_French" title="Old French">Old French</a> word <i>moschet</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Partridge1977_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Partridge1977-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> The famous list in the mediaeval <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Saint_Albans" title="Book of Saint Albans">Book of Saint Albans</a> refers to the female sparrowhawk as the "priest's bird" and the musket as "the clerk's bird".
</p><p>"An austringer [falconer] undertaking to train a sparrowhawk should be in doubt that he is taking on one of the most difficult hawks available." A female Eurasian sparrowhawk is considered a bad choice for a novice and the male is very difficult and demanding, even for an experienced handler. They have been described as "hysterical little hawks" but are also praised as courageous and providing "sport of the highest quality."<sup id="cite_ref-Ford1995_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ford1995-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> Philip Glasier describes Eurasian sparrowhawks as "in many ways superior to hunting with a larger short-wing [hawk]" and "extremely hard to tame." They are best suited for small quarry such as <a href="/wiki/Common_starling" title="Common starling">common starlings</a> and <a href="/wiki/Common_blackbird" title="Common blackbird">common blackbirds</a> but are also capable of taking <a href="/wiki/Common_teal" class="mw-redirect" title="Common teal">common teal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_magpie" title="Eurasian magpie">Eurasian magpies</a>, pheasants and partridges.<sup id="cite_ref-Glasier1986_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Glasier1986-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> A 19th century author remarked that this species was "the best of all hawks for landrails",<sup id="cite_ref-penny_67-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-penny-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> now known as <a href="/wiki/Corn_crake" title="Corn crake">corn crakes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BirdFacts_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BirdFacts-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> In 1735, the <i>Sportsman's Dictionary</i> noted that "... she will serve in the winter as well as in the summer, and will fly at all kind of game more than the falcon. If a winter sparrowhawk prove good, she will kill the <a href="/wiki/European_magpie" class="mw-redirect" title="European magpie">pye</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Red-billed_chough" title="Red-billed chough">chough</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_jay" title="Eurasian jay">jay</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_woodcock" title="Eurasian woodcock">woodcock</a>, <a href="/wiki/Turdus" class="mw-redirect" title="Turdus">thrush</a>, <a href="/wiki/Common_blackbird" title="Common blackbird">black-bird</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fieldfare" title="Fieldfare">fieldfare</a>, and divers[e] other birds of the like nature."<sup id="cite_ref-Murray1735_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Murray1735-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_culture">In culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eurasian_sparrowhawk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: In culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Accipiter_nisus_1_(Bohu%C5%A1_%C4%8C%C3%AD%C4%8Del).jpg" class="image"><img alt="Adult male Eurasian sparrowhawk perching on branch" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Accipiter_nisus_1_%28Bohu%C5%A1_%C4%8C%C3%AD%C4%8Del%29.jpg/220px-Accipiter_nisus_1_%28Bohu%C5%A1_%C4%8C%C3%AD%C4%8Del%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="330" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Accipiter_nisus_1_%28Bohu%C5%A1_%C4%8C%C3%AD%C4%8Del%29.jpg/330px-Accipiter_nisus_1_%28Bohu%C5%A1_%C4%8C%C3%AD%C4%8Del%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Accipiter_nisus_1_%28Bohu%C5%A1_%C4%8C%C3%AD%C4%8Del%29.jpg/440px-Accipiter_nisus_1_%28Bohu%C5%A1_%C4%8C%C3%AD%C4%8Del%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="682" data-file-height="1024" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Accipiter_nisus_1_(Bohu%C5%A1_%C4%8C%C3%AD%C4%8Del).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An adult male in Slovakia</div></div></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Slavic_mythology" class="mw-redirect" title="Slavic mythology">Slavic mythology</a>, the sparrowhawk, known as <i>krahui</i> or <i>krahug</i>, is a sacred bird in Old Bohemian songs and lives in a grove of the gods. Holy sparrowhawks perch on the branches of an oak tree that grows from the grave of a murdered man, and "publish the foul deed."<sup id="cite_ref-Grimm2003_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Grimm2003-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> In some areas of England, it was believed that the <a href="/wiki/Common_cuckoo" title="Common cuckoo">common cuckoo</a> turned into a Eurasian sparrowhawk in winter.<sup id="cite_ref-Spence1979_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Spence1979-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Surname" title="Surname">name</a> <i>Spearhafoc</i> (later Sparhawk, Sparrowhawk) was in use as a personal name in England before the <a href="/wiki/Norman_conquest_of_England" title="Norman conquest of England">Norman conquest</a> in 1066.<sup id="cite_ref-Reaney1997_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reaney1997-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1695, John Aubrey wrote in his <i>Miscellanies</i>:<sup id="cite_ref-lore_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lore-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<blockquote><p>Not long before the Death of King Charles II a sparrow-hawk escaped from the Perch, and pitched on one of the Iron Crowns of the White Tower, and entangling its string in the Crown, hung by the heels and died. T'was considered very ominous, and so it proved.</p></blockquote>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Musket" title="Musket">musket</a>, or musquet, originally a kind of <a href="/wiki/Crossbow" title="Crossbow">crossbow</a> bolt,<sup id="cite_ref-brit_61-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brit-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> and later a small <a href="/wiki/Cannon" title="Cannon">cannon</a>, was named after the male Eurasian sparrowhawk because of its size.<sup id="cite_ref-Stone1999_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stone1999-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> The British <a href="/wiki/Gloster_Aircraft_Company" title="Gloster Aircraft Company">Gloster Aircraft Company</a> named one of their Mars series craft<sup id="cite_ref-mda.org.uk_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mda.org.uk-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Gloster_Sparrowhawk" title="Gloster Sparrowhawk">Sparrowhawk</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-brit_61-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brit-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/William_Shakespeare" title="William Shakespeare">William Shakespeare</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Merry_Wives_of_Windsor" title="The Merry Wives of Windsor">The Merry Wives of Windsor</a></i>, Mrs Ford greets Robin, <a href="/wiki/Falstaff" class="mw-redirect" title="Falstaff">Falstaff</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Page_(servant)" title="Page (servant)">page</a>, with the words "How now, my eyas musket", <i>eyas musket</i> meaning a lively young man (an eyas is a hawk nestling).<sup id="cite_ref-Harting1965_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harting1965-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Poet_Laureate_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Poet Laureate of the United Kingdom">British Poet Laureate</a> <a href="/wiki/Ted_Hughes" title="Ted Hughes">Ted Hughes</a> wrote a poem entitled "A Sparrow-Hawk" which refers to this species.<sup id="cite_ref-Gifford2009_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gifford2009-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hermann_Hesse" title="Hermann Hesse">Hermann Hesse</a> mentioned this bird in his book <i><a href="/wiki/Demian" title="Demian">Demian</a></i><sup id="cite_ref-Demian_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Demian-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> and the bird is also referred to in <i><a href="/wiki/One_Thousand_and_One_Nights" title="One Thousand and One Nights">One Thousand and One Nights</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Richard_Francis_Burton" title="Richard Francis Burton">Richard Francis Burton</a>:<sup id="cite_ref-Burton_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Burton-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<blockquote><p>Good sooth my bones, wheneas they hear thy name<br />
Quail as birds quailed when Nisus o'er them flew<br /></p></blockquote>
<p>The Eurasian sparrowhawk was written about by <a href="/wiki/Alfred,_Lord_Tennyson" title="Alfred, Lord Tennyson">Alfred, Lord Tennyson</a>:<sup id="cite_ref-lore_5-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lore-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<blockquote><p>A sparhawk proud did hold in wicked jail<br />
</p><p>Music's sweet chorister, the Nightingale<br />
To whom with sighs she said: 'O set me free,<br />
And in my song I'll praise no bird but thee.'<br />
The Hawk replied: 'I will not lose my diet<br />
</p><p>
To let a thousand such enjoy their quiet.'<br /></p></blockquote>
<p><br />
In Ursula K. Le Guin <a href="/wiki/Earthsea" title="Earthsea">Earthsea</a> novels, the main character is a boy called Duny who is nicknamed Sparrowhawk.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eurasian_sparrowhawk&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 35em; -webkit-column-width: 35em; column-width: 35em; list-style-type: decimal;">

<li id="cite_note-IUCN-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-IUCN_1-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-IUCN_1-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite id="CITEREFBirdLife_International2013" class="citation journal cs1"><a href="/wiki/BirdLife_International" title="BirdLife International">BirdLife International</a> (2013). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.iucnredlist.org/details/22695624/0">"<i>Accipiter nisus</i>"</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/IUCN_Red_List" title="IUCN Red List">IUCN Red List of Threatened Species</a></i>. <b>2013</b><span class="reference-accessdate">.
